<meta name="DC.Title" content="Early Sex-Chromosome Evolution in the Diploid Dioecious Plant Mercurialis annua" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1534/genetics.119.302045" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2019-07-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="Genetics" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="Copyright © 2019 by the Genetics Society of America. Available freely online through the author-supported open access option." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Relation" content="10.1534/genetics.120.303159" />
<meta name="DC.Relation" content="10.1534/genetics.120.303159" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Suppressed recombination allows divergence between homologous sex chromosomes and the functionality of their genes. Here, we reveal patterns of the earliest stages of sex-chromosome evolution in the diploid dioecious herb Mercurialis annua on the basis of cytological analysis, de novo genome assembly and annotation, genetic mapping, exome resequencing of natural populations, and transcriptome analysis. The genome assembly contained 34,105 expressed genes, of which 10,076 were assigned to linkage groups. Genetic mapping and exome resequencing of individuals across the species range both identified the largest linkage group, LG1, as the sex chromosome. Although the sex chromosomes of M. annua are karyotypically homomorphic, we estimate that about one-third of the Y chromosome, containing 568 transcripts and spanning 22.3 cM in the corresponding female map, has ceased recombining. Nevertheless, we found limited evidence for Y-chromosome degeneration in terms of gene loss and pseudogenization, and most X- and Y-linked genes appear to have diverged in the period subsequent to speciation between M. annua and its sister species M. huetii , which shares the same sex-determining region. Taken together, our results suggest that the M. annua Y chromosome has at least two evolutionary strata: a small old stratum shared with M. huetii , and a more recent larger stratum that is probably unique to M. annua and that stopped recombining ∼1 MYA. Patterns of gene expression within the nonrecombining region are consistent with the idea that sexually antagonistic selection may have played a role in favoring suppressed recombination." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Early Sex-Chromosome Evolution in the Diploid Dioecious Plant Mercurialis annua" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Suppressed recombination allows divergence between homologous sex chromosomes and the functionality of their genes. Here, we reveal patterns of the earliest stages of sex-chromosome evolution in the diploid dioecious herb &lt;i&gt;Mercurialis annua&lt;/i&gt; on the basis of cytological analysis, &lt;i&gt;de novo&lt;/i&gt; genome assembly and annotation, genetic mapping, exome resequencing of natural populations, and transcriptome analysis. The genome assembly contained 34,105 expressed genes, of which 10,076 were assigned to linkage groups. Genetic mapping and exome resequencing of individuals across the species range both identified the largest linkage group, LG1, as the sex chromosome. Although the sex chromosomes of &lt;i&gt;M. annua&lt;/i&gt; are karyotypically homomorphic, we estimate that about one-third of the Y chromosome, containing 568 transcripts and spanning 22.3&amp;nbsp;cM in the corresponding female map, has ceased recombining. Nevertheless, we found limited evidence for Y-chromosome degeneration in terms of gene loss and pseudogenization, and most X- and Y-linked genes appear to have diverged in the period subsequent to speciation between &lt;i&gt;M. annua&lt;/i&gt; and its sister species &lt;i&gt;M. huetii&lt;/i&gt;, which shares the same sex-determining region. Taken together, our results suggest that the &lt;i&gt;M. annua&lt;/i&gt; Y chromosome has at least two evolutionary strata: a small old stratum shared with &lt;i&gt;M. huetii&lt;/i&gt;, and a more recent larger stratum that is probably unique to &lt;i&gt;M. annua&lt;/i&gt; and that stopped recombining ∼1&amp;nbsp;MYA. Patterns of gene expression within the nonrecombining region are consistent with the idea that sexually antagonistic selection may have played a role in favoring suppressed recombination.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Early Sex-Chromosome Evolution in the Diploid Dioecious Plant Mercurialis annua" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.genetics.org/sites/default/files/highwire/genetics/212/3.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Suppressed recombination allows divergence between homologous sex chromosomes and the functionality of their genes. Here, we reveal patterns of the earliest stages of sex-chromosome evolution in the diploid dioecious herb Mercurialis annua on the basis of cytological analysis, de novo genome assembly and annotation, genetic mapping, exome resequencing of natural populations, and transcriptome analysis. The genome assembly contained 34,105 expressed genes, of which 10,076 were assigned to linkage groups. Genetic mapping and exome resequencing of individuals across the species range both identified the largest linkage group, LG1, as the sex chromosome. Although the sex chromosomes of M. annua are karyotypically homomorphic, we estimate that about one-third of the Y chromosome, containing 568 transcripts and spanning 22.3 cM in the corresponding female map, has ceased recombining. Nevertheless, we found limited evidence for Y-chromosome degeneration in terms of gene loss and pseudogenization, and most X- and Y-linked genes appear to have diverged in the period subsequent to speciation between M. annua and its sister species M. huetii , which shares the same sex-determining region. Taken together, our results suggest that the M. annua Y chromosome has at least two evolutionary strata: a small old stratum shared with M. huetii , and a more recent larger stratum that is probably unique to M. annua and that stopped recombining ∼1 MYA. Patterns of gene expression within the nonrecombining region are consistent with the idea that sexually antagonistic selection may have played a role in favoring suppressed recombination." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Suppressed recombination allows divergence between homologous sex chromosomes and the functionality of their genes. Here, we reveal patterns of the earliest stages of sex-chromosome evolution in the diploid dioecious herb Mercurialis annua on the basis of cytological analysis, de novo genome assembly and annotation, genetic mapping, exome resequencing of natural populations, and transcriptome analysis. The genome assembly contained 34,105 expressed genes, of which 10,076 were assigned to linkage groups. Genetic mapping and exome resequencing of individuals across the species range both identified the largest linkage group, LG1, as the sex chromosome. Although the sex chromosomes of M. annua are karyotypically homomorphic, we estimate that about one-third of the Y chromosome, containing 568 transcripts and spanning 22.3 cM in the corresponding female map, has ceased recombining. Nevertheless, we found limited evidence for Y-chromosome degeneration in terms of gene loss and pseudogenization, and most X- and Y-linked genes appear to have diverged in the period subsequent to speciation between M. annua and its sister species M. huetii , which shares the same sex-determining region. Taken together, our results suggest that the M. annua Y chromosome has at least two evolutionary strata: a small old stratum shared with M. huetii , and a more recent larger stratum that is probably unique to M. annua and that stopped recombining ∼1 MYA. Patterns of gene expression within the nonrecombining region are consistent with the idea that sexually antagonistic selection may have played a role in favoring suppressed recombination." />

B. Marais</a></li></ul></div><div class="author-tooltip-11"><div class="author-tooltip-name">Dmitry A. Filatov </div><div class="author-tooltip-affiliation"><span class="author-tooltip-text"><div class='author-affiliation'><span class='nlm-sup'>‡</span>Department of Plant Sciences, University of Oxford, OX1 3RB, United Kingdom</div></span></div><ul class="author-tooltip-find-more"><li class="author-tooltip-gs-link first"><a href="/lookup/google-scholar?link_type=googlescholar&amp;gs_type=author&amp;author%5B0%5D=Dmitry%2BA.%2BFilatov%2B" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on Google Scholar</a></li><li class="author-tooltip-pubmed-link"><a href="/lookup/external-ref?access_num=Filatov%20DA&amp;link_type=AUTHORSEARCH" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on PubMed</a></li><li class="author-site-search-link last"><a href="/search/author1%3ADmitry%2BA.%2BFilatov%2B" rel="nofollow" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Search for this author on this site</a></li></ul></div><div class="author-tooltip-12"><div class="author-tooltip-name">John R. Pannell </div><div class="author-tooltip-affiliation"><span class="author-tooltip-text"><div class='author-affiliation'><span class='nlm-sup'>†</span>Department of Ecology and Evolution, University of Lausanne, CH-1015, Switzerland</div></span></div><ul class="author-tooltip-find-more"><li class="author-tooltip-gs-link first"><a href="/lookup/google-scholar?link_type=googlescholar&amp;gs_type=author&amp;author%5B0%5D=John%2BR.%2BPannell%2B" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on Google Scholar</a></li><li class="author-tooltip-pubmed-link"><a href="/lookup/external-ref?access_num=Pannell%20JR&amp;link_type=AUTHORSEARCH" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on PubMed</a></li><li class="author-site-search-link"><a href="/search/author1%3AJohn%2BR.%2BPannell%2B" rel="nofollow" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Search for this author on this site</a></li><li class="author-orcid-link"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-0098-7074" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">ORCID record for John R. Pannell</a></li><li class="author-corresp-email-link last"><span>For correspondence:

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container article-page-ajax-tab" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_genetics_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.genetics.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_genetics_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-corrections" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="messages highwire-corrections highwire-correction-type-correction"><h3 class="highwire-corrections-title">This article has a correction. Please see:</h3><ul class="highwire-corrections-list"><li class="relation-67969 first last"><a href="/content/214/4/1121" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Early Sex-Chromosome Evolution in the Diploid Dioecious Plant Mercurialis annua - April 01, 2020</a></li></ul></div>  </div>

  
  </div>
<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup article-page-highwire" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-1"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-6">Suppressed recombination allows divergence between homologous sex chromosomes and the functionality of their genes. Here, we reveal patterns of the earliest stages of sex-chromosome evolution in the diploid dioecious herb <em>Mercurialis annua</em> on the basis of cytological analysis, <em>de novo</em> genome assembly and annotation, genetic mapping, exome resequencing of natural populations, and transcriptome analysis. The genome assembly contained 34,105 expressed genes, of which 10,076 were assigned to linkage groups. Genetic mapping and exome resequencing of individuals across the species range both identified the largest linkage group, LG1, as the sex chromosome. Although the sex chromosomes of <em>M. annua</em> are karyotypically homomorphic, we estimate that about one-third of the Y chromosome, containing 568 transcripts and spanning 22.3 cM in the corresponding female map, has ceased recombining. Nevertheless, we found limited evidence for Y-chromosome degeneration in terms of gene loss and pseudogenization, and most X- and Y-linked genes appear to have diverged in the period subsequent to speciation between <em>M. annua</em> and its sister species <em>M. huetii</em>, which shares the same sex-determining region. Taken together, our results suggest that the <em>M. annua</em> Y chromosome has at least two evolutionary strata: a small old stratum shared with <em>M. huetii</em>, and a more recent larger stratum that is probably unique to <em>M. annua</em> and that stopped recombining ∼1 MYA. Patterns of gene expression within the nonrecombining region are consistent with the idea that sexually antagonistic selection may have played a role in favoring suppressed recombination.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/sex-chromosomes" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-sex-chromosomes" rel="nofollow">sex chromosomes</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/whole-genome-sequencing-0" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-whole-genome-sequencing" rel="nofollow">whole genome sequencing</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/sex-linkage" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-sex-linkage" rel="nofollow">sex linkage</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/evolutionary-strata" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-evolutionary-strata" rel="nofollow">evolutionary strata</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/gene-expression" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-gene-expression" rel="nofollow">gene expression</a></li></ul><p id="p-7">THE evolution of dioecy (separate sexes) from hermaphroditism, which has occurred repeatedly in independent lineages of flowering plants (<a id="xref-ref-99-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Renner 2014</a>), is a prelude to the possible evolution of sex chromosomes. Early sex-chromosome evolution typically involves the accumulation of repetitive sequences in a nonrecombining region (<a id="xref-ref-120-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-120">J. Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Hobza <em>et al.</em> 2017</a>), differences in codon use between homologs (<a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">Ono 1939</a>; <a id="xref-ref-93-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">Qiu <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>), different patterns of gene expression at sex-linked loci (<a id="xref-ref-131-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-131">Zemp <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>), pseudogenization and gene loss (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Papadopulos <em>et al.</em> 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-125-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-125">Wu and Moore 2015</a>), and, ultimately, divergence in chromosome length between homologs (<a id="xref-ref-92-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">Puterova <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>). Extreme divergence is common in many animals, but it is also known in some plants in which the homologous chromosomes are heteromorphic and distinguishable by karyotype, <em>e.g.</em>, in the plant species <em>Silene latifolia</em> (<a id="xref-ref-82-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">Ono 1939</a>; <a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Krasovec <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>) and <em>Rumex hastatulus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-110-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-110">Smith 1955</a>; <a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Hough <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>). In other plants, the sex chromosomes remain indistinguishable by karyotype, and gene function is only mildly compromised, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Asparagus officinalis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Loeptien 1979</a>; <a id="xref-ref-113-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-113">Telgmann-Rauber <em>et al.</em> 2007</a>), <em>Spinacia oleracea</em> (<a id="xref-ref-129-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-129">Yamamoto <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>), <em>Diospyros lotus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Akagi <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>), <em>Fragaria chiloensis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-114-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-114">Tennessen <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>), <em>Populus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Geraldes <em>et al.</em> 2015</a>), <em>Carica papaya</em> (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Horovitz and Jiménez 1967</a>; <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2004</a>), and <em>Salix</em> (<a id="xref-ref-90-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-90">Pucholt <em>et al.</em> 2015</a>). Because of this variation, and because dioecy in plants has often evolved recently, plants with young homomorphic sex chromosomes provide particularly good models for studying the very earliest stages in sex-chromosome divergence (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Charlesworth 2016</a>).</p><p id="p-8">Two hypotheses have been proposed to explain the suppression of recombination in plants. First, if dioecy evolves through the spread of male- and female-sterility mutations, these mutations must become linked on opposite chromosomes to avoid the expression of either hermaphroditism, or both male and female sterility simultaneously (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Charlesworth and Charlesworth 1978</a>). The main experimental support for this two-locus model comes from classic genetic studies in <em>S. latifolia</em> (<a id="xref-ref-122-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-122">Westergaard 1958</a>) that demonstrated the presence of two sex-determining factors on the Y-chromosome: the stamen-promoting factor (SPF) and gynoecium suppression factor (GSF). More recent work mapped the location of these genes on the <em>S. latifolia</em> Y-chromosome (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Kazama <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>), although the actual GSF and SPF genes have yet to be identified. Nevertheless, although there is some support for it, the two-locus model does not explain why nonrecombining regions on sex chromosomes often expand greatly (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Bergero and Charlesworth 2009</a>), well beyond the region harboring the original sex-determining genes.</p><p id="p-9">A second hypothesis invokes selection favoring suppressed recombination between a sex-determining locus and loci elsewhere on the sex chromosome that have different allelic effects on the fitness of males and females, <em>i.e.</em>, alleles with sexually antagonistic effects (<a id="xref-ref-100-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">Rice 1987</a>; <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Charlesworth 1991</a>; <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Gibson <em>et al.</em> 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Charlesworth <em>et al.</em> 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Bergero and Charlesworth 2009</a>). The suppression of recombination is expected to extend consecutively to generate linkage between the sex-determining locus and more sexually antagonistic loci (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Charlesworth 2015</a>), and these extensions can be identified as discrete “strata,” with greater X/Y divergence in strata that ceased recombining earliest. Evidence for strata has been found both in animals (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Lahn and Page 1999</a>; <a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">Nam and Ellegren 2008</a>) and plants (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Bergero <em>et al.</em> 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-120-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-120">K. Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>), but there is still little direct evidence for the role played by sexually antagonistic selection in bringing them about (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Bergero <em>et al.</em> 2019</a>). A recent study of guppy sex chromosomes claimed evidence for the evolution of strata consistent with the sexual-antagonism hypothesis (<a id="xref-ref-123-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-123">Wright <em>et al.</em> 2017</a>), but subsequent work has shown that strata could actually not have been involved in the evolution of suppressed recombination (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Bergero <em>et al.</em> 2019</a>). Although our understanding of the implications of suppressed recombination is well developed, evidence for its driver therefore remains weak.</p><p id="p-10">Sexually antagonistic selection may be resolved either through differential gene expression between males and females, or through sex linkage of the responsible loci, with the phenotypic expression of sexual dimorphism being the ultimate result. Sexual dimorphism is known in dioecious species for a wide range of morphological (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Eckhart 1999</a>), life-history (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Delph 1999</a>) and physiological traits (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Dawson and Geber 1999</a>), as well as for gene expression (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Baker <em>et al.</em> 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-106-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-106">Sharma <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">Mohanty <em>et al.</em> 2017</a>; <a id="xref-ref-103-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-103">Sanderson <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>). In some cases, the differentially expressed genes are enriched on the sex chromosomes (<a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Parisi <em>et al.</em> 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Leder <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Albritton <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-91-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">Pucholt <em>et al.</em> 2017</a>), likely as a result of the degeneration of one of the diploid copies following the suppression of recombination, or due to interactions between sex and gene expression at the loci concerned (reviewed in <a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">Mank and Ellegren 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">Parsch and Ellegren 2013</a>). In the latter scenario, dosage compensation may subsequently evolve in response to selection to restore similar levels of expression in males and females. While dosage compensation is an important feature of gene expression in many animal lineages (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Mank 2013</a>), its extent in plants is understudied. The clearest analysis remains that for <em>S. latifolia</em>, in which <a id="xref-ref-86-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Papadopulos <em>et al.</em> (2015)</a> confirmed gene loss, or lost expression, for many Y-linked genes, with associated incomplete dosage compensation from X-linked homologs, including full compensation at some loci. Further study of sequence evolution and patterns of gene expression in other species would be valuable, particularly those with homomorphic sex chromosomes at the very earliest stages of their evolution, as well as for loci close to the sex-determining locus.</p><p id="p-11">Here, we identify the homomorphic sex chromosomes of the wind-pollinated dioecious annual plant <em>Mercurialis annua</em> (Euphorbiacae) using genetic mapping, sequence analysis of the genome, and patterns of gene expression. Until recently, sex in dioecious <em>M. annua</em> was thought to be determined by allelic variation at three independent loci (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Durand <em>et al.</em> 1987</a>; <a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Durand and Durand 1991</a>), but it is now known to have a simple XY system (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Khadka <em>et al.</em> 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-102-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">Russell and Pannell 2015</a>). YY males of <em>M. annua</em>, which lack an X chromosome, are viable but partially sterile (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Kuhn 1939</a>; <a id="xref-ref-131-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-131">Li <em>et al.</em> 2019</a>), indicating that the Y chromosome is only mildly degenerate. Our present study confirms this suggestion, finding very limited gene loss, yet suppressed recombination over a large portion of the sex chromosomes and substantial differences in gene expression between males and females at sex-linked loci. It would thus appear that the sex chromosomes of the species may be at a particularly interesting and revealing stage in their evolution (<a id="xref-ref-118-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-118">Veltsos <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>).</p><p id="p-12">To assemble the genome of <em>M. annua</em>, we combined short-read sequencing on the Illumina platform with long-read technology developed by Pacific Biosciences. We also analyzed the karyotype of <em>M. annua</em> using current imaging technology in an attempt to identify heteromorphism at the cytological level, and we obtained SNPs from the transcriptomes of small families to construct a genetic map for <em>M. annua</em>, to identify nonrecombining (sex-linked) genes and scaffolds, and to compare them with non-sex-linked regions. We then sought evidence for the evolution of evolutionary strata and genetic degeneration on the Y chromosome, including the fixation of pseudogenising mutations in the inferred fully Y-linked sequences, and the proportion of genes deleted from the Y. Finally, we examined sex-biased gene expression, and assessed whether sex-biased genes might be enriched on the sex chromosomes, as expected by theory (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Connallon and Clark 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">Meisel <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). Our results suggest that the sex chromosomes of <em>M. annua</em> are ∼1.5 million years old. Early stages of Y-chromosome degeneration are clearly apparent, but there are also signs that patterns of gene expression might have been affected by the accumulation of sexually antagonistic mutations.</p><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Hairy root culture, chromosome preparation, and cytogenetics</h3><p id="p-13"><em>M. annua</em> seeds were sterilized by incubation in 4% sodium hypochloride and subsequently washed in 50% ethanol and sterile water. Seeds were grown on MS medium, and leaf discs were taken from 2- to 3-week-old plants that had been sexed using a previously developed Sequence Characterized Amplified Region (SCAR) marker (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Khadka <em>et al.</em> 2002</a>). <em>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</em> strain ARqua1 (<a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">Quandt <em>et al.</em> 1993</a>) was grown overnight at 28° in LB medium supplemented with 300 µM acetosyringone to OD<sub>600</sub> = 0.6. The culture was resuspended in liquid 1/2 MS with 300 µM acetosyringone, and used for direct inoculation of <em>M. annua</em> leaf discs. Explants were cocultivated at 28° on MS medium with 300 µM acetosyringone for 2 days. After cocultivation, explants were moved to MS medium supplemented with 300 µg/liter Timentin. Media were changed every 2 weeks.</p><p id="p-14">To synchronize the hairy roots of <em>M. annua</em>, the DNA polymerase inhibitor aphidicolin was added for 12 hr. Mitoses were then accumulated in protoplasts using oryzalin treatment, transferred to chromic-acid-washed slides by dropping, and stored at −20° until use. For fluorescence <em>in situ</em> hybridization (FISH) experiments, slides were denatured in 7:3 (v/v) formamide: 2xSSC for 2 min at 72°, immediately dehydrated through 50, 70, and 100% ethanol (−20°), and air dried. The hybridization mixture (30 µl per slide) consisted of 200 ng of labeled probe, 15 µl formamide, 6 µl 50% dextrane sulfate, and 3 µl of 20× SSC. The volume was brought to 30 µl by adding TE, pH 8. The probes were denatured at 70° for 10 min, and slides hybridized for 18 hr at 37° in a humid chamber. Slides were analyzed using an Olympus Provis AX70 microscope, and image analysis was performed using ISIS software (Metasystems). DNA was labeled with Fluorolink Cy3-dUTP (red labeling; Amersham Pharmacia Biotech) using a Nick Translation mix (Roche), or with SpectrumGreen direct-labeled dUTP (green labeling; Vysis) and a Nick Translation kit (Vysis).</p></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Genome and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p id="p-15">DNA for genomic sequencing was taken from leaf tissue of a single male, M1. RNA samples were collected from leaves and flower buds of this individual, as well as from three unrelated females, G1, G2, and G3, and two additional unrelated males, M2 and M3, all sampled from north-western France and grown together in a glasshouse. Sixty-five F<sub>1</sub> and F<sub>2</sub> progeny (five families) were then produced by crossing G1xM1 and G2xM1 (F<sub>1</sub>) as well as three pairs of G1xM1 progeny (F<sub>2</sub>) (Supplemental Material, Table S1), which were also used for RNA extraction and transcriptome sequencing. DNA was extracted using a Qiagen Plant DNeasy kit (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany). Illumina paired-end and mate-pair sequencing was carried out by the Beijing Genomics Institute (BGI) using Illumina HiSeq 2000 technology (100 bp reads). Pacific Biosciences long-read sequencing was performed on the individual M1 by the Centre for Integrative Genomics hosted at the University of Lausanne. RNA was extracted from a mixture of flower buds and leaf tissues using the Qiagen plant RNAeasy kit (Qiagen), and individual libraries were prepared for all 65 individuals (Table S1), which were sequenced on three lanes of Illumina HiSeq 2000 at the Wellcome Trust Centre for Human Genetics, Oxford.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Transcriptome assembly and annotation</h3><p id="p-16">The SEX-DETector pipeline (<a href="http://lbbe.univ-lyon1.fr/-SEX-DETector-.html?lang=fr">http://lbbe.univ-lyon1.fr/-SEX-DETector-.html?lang=fr</a>) was used to produce the reference transcriptome (henceforth “ORFs”), and to obtain allele-specific expression and SNPs for X and Y haplotypes. Sex linkage was inferred from genetic mapping (see below). To identify sex-linked haplotypes, we employed a probabilistic model based on maximum-likelihood inference, implemented in SEX-DETector (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Muyle <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>). SEX-DETector, which assumes that X-linked and Y-linked haplotypes are passed only from fathers to daughters or from fathers to sons, respectively, is embedded into a Galaxy workflow pipeline that includes extra-assembly, mapping and genotyping steps prior to sex-linkage inference, which has been shown to have greater sensitivity, without an increased false positive rate, than without these steps (<a id="xref-ref-78-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Muyle <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>). First, poly-A tails were removed from transcripts using PRINSEQ (<a id="xref-ref-105-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-105">Schmieder and Edwards 2011</a>), with parameters -trim_tail_left 5 -trim_tail_right 5. rRNA-like sequences were removed using riboPicker version 0.4.3 (<a id="xref-ref-105-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-105">Schmieder and Edwards 2011</a>) with parameters -i 90 -c 50 -l 50 and the following databases: SILVA Large subunit reference database, SILVA Small subunit reference database, the GreenGenes database, and the Rfam database. Transcripts were then further assembled within Trinity components using cap3 (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Huang and Madan 1999</a>), with parameter -p 90 and custom Perl scripts. Coding sequences were predicted using Trinity TransDecoder (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Haas <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>), including PFAM domain searches as ORF retention criteria; these sequences were taken as our reference transcriptome. The RNAseq reads from parents and progeny were mapped onto the reference transcriptome using BWA (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Li and Durbin 2009</a>). The alignments were analyzed using reads2snp, a genotyper for RNAseq data that gives better results than standard genotypers when X and Y transcripts have different expression levels (<a id="xref-ref-117-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-117">Tsagkogeorga <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>).</p><p id="p-17">The SEX-DETector transcripts were mapped onto the genome using GMAP v2017-06-20 (<a id="xref-ref-126-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-126">Wu and Watanabe 2005</a>), disallowing chimeric genes, mapping 38,963 (99%) of them to 48,298 loci. We then collapsed the inferred ORFs into 34,105 gene models using gffread v0.9.9 (<a href="http://ccb.jhu.edu/software/stringtie/gff.shtml">http://ccb.jhu.edu/software/stringtie/gff.shtml</a>). In total, 26,702 (67.9%) of the ORFs could be annotated using fast blastx on the Swiss-Prot database and Blast2GO (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Conesa <em>et al.</em> 2005</a>) in Geneious v9 (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Kearse <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). The resulting transcriptome annotation information, including GO terms, is presented in File S4. We characterized genes as candidate sex-determining genes by identifying those containing the following strings in their annotation: flower, auxin, cytokinin, pollen, kelch, ethylene, swi, retinol, calmodulin, short-root, jasmonic. This manual characterization allowed us to visualize the mapping locations, test for their enrichment in the nonrecombining region, and identify whether any were among the outliers in various transcript-associated metrics.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Genome size estimation, assembly, and annotation</h3><p id="p-18">The genome size was estimated by fitting the k-mer distribution of the raw reads, obtained from jellyfish v2.1.0 (<a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Marçais and Kingsford 2011</a>), using GenomeScope v2 (<a href="https://github.com/tbenavi1/genomescope/tree/9eaab4b27dccad6c8bf4e3a5883271b943412ff2">https://github.com/tbenavi1/genomescope/tree/9eaab4b27dccad6c8bf4e3a5883271b943412ff2</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-119-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-119">Vurture <em>et al.</em> 2017</a>), after accounting for the expected coverage of a haploid genome of 10 (genomescope.R -i fastq_countsM1_31.histo -o M1_31_prior_tr -l 10 -p 2 -k 31 —transform).</p><p id="p-19">Sliding-window trimming and adaptor removal were carried out using Trimmomatic v. 0.30, with default parameters (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Bolger <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>). Exact duplicate read pairs were collapsed using fastx-collapser from the Fastx-Toolkit (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/</a>). Low-complexity masking was carried out using DUST (<a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Morgulis <em>et al.</em> 2006</a>), with the default parameters, and ambiguous reads were removed. Pacific Biosciences long reads were error-corrected using Bowtie2 version 2.1.0 (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Langmead and Salzberg 2012</a>) in combination with LSC version 0.3.1 (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Au <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>).</p><p id="p-20">Filtered paired-end and mate-paired genomic reads were assembled using SOAPdenovo2 (<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Luo <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>), with k-mer values between 35 and 55 (odd values only). The best assembly was chosen using REAPR (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Hunt <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). GapCloser (<a id="xref-ref-69-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Luo <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>) was run on the best assembly to correct false joins and fill gaps. Error-corrected Pacific Biosciences reads were then used to extend scaffolds, to fill gaps, and to join scaffolds using PBJelly2 (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">English <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). Additional scaffolding was carried out using default parameters on SSPACE (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Boetzer <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>), which revisits gaps using existing paired-end and mate-paired sequences. This step is intended to correct for any ambiguity introduced by the low-coverage of PacBio reads. Finally, L_RNA_Scaffolder (<a id="xref-ref-128-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-128">Xue <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>) was used to bridge genomic scaffolds using the transcript assembly.</p><p id="p-21">Transposable elements (TEs) and tandem repeats were predicted using a combination of Tandem Repeats Finder (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Benson 1999</a>), RepeatModeler, and RepeatMasker (<a id="xref-ref-109-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-109">Smit <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). Repeat libraries from <em>M. annua</em> (from RepeatModeler), Euphorbiacae, and <em>Vitis vinifera</em> were used for masking the genome before further analyses.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Summary of SNP calling pipelines</h3><p id="p-22">We called SNPs using four different pipelines. (1) X- and Y-specific SNPs were called and haplotypes with premature stop codons, and allele-specific expression SNPs were inferred on the basis of RNAseq data from the two largest families by SEX-DETector. (2) SNPs were called from genome-capture data of multiple populations, for independent analysis of genomic regions of differing sex linkage, as inferred from the genetic map. (3) SNPs were called on all individuals from five families, to perform genetic mapping. (4) SNPs were called on transcripts from the three unrelated males (M1, M2, and M3) and the three unrelated females (G1, G2, and G3) for comparisons between ORFs of differing sex linkage and comparisons of pairwise diversity between species. The SNPs for which there was evidence of separate X and Y copies and which also had homologs in <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R. communis</em> were analyzed in a phylogenetic framework to estimate the (tree-based) evolution rate of the Y sequences. Specific details of each pipeline are given in the corresponding sections below.</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Genetic map construction</h3><p id="p-23">We called SNPs independently of the SEX-DETector pipeline, using all individuals available over the three generations of our family crosses. We first mapped RNAseq reads to the reference transcriptome using Bowtie2 v2.3.1 (<a id="xref-ref-61-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Langmead and Salzberg 2012</a>). The resulting sam files were converted to bam, sorted, and converted to mpileup format using samtools v 1.3 (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Li <em>et al.</em> 2009</a>). The resulting posterior file containing segregation information of transcripts in the five mapping families (&gt;164,000 markers and their genotype likelihoods) was passed through the LepMap3 (LM3) pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-96-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">Rastas 2017</a>). First, we calculated the relatedness between individuals using the IBD module (of LM3) using a random subset of 3000 markers. Three individuals were discarded because their relatedness to their putative father (M6) was &lt;0.2, <em>i.e.</em>, they were likely the result of a different cross through contamination. The parental genotypes were then called using ParentCall2, with parameter halfSibs = 1, to take into account the half-sib family structure, in addition to the genotypic information of offspring, parents, and grandparents. The 158,000 remaining markers were further filtered using the Filtering2 module, with parameter dataTolerance = 0.001, to remove markers with distorted segregating. We then combined markers of each transcript into pseudomarkers, as they were not sufficiently informative on their own to separate into linkage groups. This was done by running OrderMarkers2 separately for each transcript without recombination (parameter values: recombination1 = 0, recombination2 = 0), and then obtaining genotype likelihoods for each transcript with parameter outputPhasedData = 4. This resulted in 13,261 markers, each consisting of a single transcript, which we reformatted as input for LM3. We defined linkage groups by running SeparateChromosomes2 with default parameters (lodLimit = 10), adding additional markers to the resulting eight linkage groups using JoinSingles2 with lodLimit = 8 and lodDifference = 2. We were able to assign 10,076 transcripts to eight linkage groups, which were ordered using OrderMarkers2. The linkage maps were drawn with R/qtl v1.41 (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">Broman <em>et al.</em> 2003</a>) running in R 3.4.2 (<a id="xref-ref-98-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-98">R Development Core Team 2007</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Sequence divergence in <em>M. annua</em>, and in comparison with closely related species</h3><p id="p-24">We aligned reads from six unrelated individuals from France (M1, M2, M3, G1, G2, G3) to the 39,302 ORFs from the reference transcriptome assembled via the SEX-DETector pipeline, based on the M1 male transcriptome and using the BWA-MEM algorithm of BWA v0.7.13 (<a id="xref-ref-63-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Li and Durbin 2009</a>). Picard Tools v2.2.1 (<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>) was used to mark duplicate read pairs. Local realignment around insertions and deletions (indels) was performed with GATK v3.7 (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">DePristo <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>), followed by SNP calling on each individual using the HaplotypeCaller module in GATK (<a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>). Joint SNP calling was performed using GATK’s GenotypeGVCFs module, overlapping SNPs and indels were filtered out with the VariantFiltration module, and SNPs and indels were separated and filtered to produce two high-quality variant sets with the following parameters in the VariantFiltration module: “QUAL &lt;30” “DP &lt; 30” “MQ0 &gt;= 4 &amp;&amp; ((MQ0/(1.0 * DP)) &gt; 0.1)” “QD &lt; 5.0”. The high-quality SNP set was used to perform variant quality score recalibration to filter the full SNP set. This set was used to calculate within-species nucleotide diversity (<em>π</em>) using vcftools v 0.1.13 (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Danecek <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>).</p><p id="p-25">Pairwise d<sub>N</sub> and d<sub>S</sub> values from <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R</em>. <em>communis</em> were obtained using yn00 from PAML 4.9 (<a id="xref-ref-130-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-130">Yang 2007</a>). This analysis used 4761 and 2993 homologs from the <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R. communis</em> (<a href="http://castorbean.jcvi.org/introduction.shtml">http://castorbean.jcvi.org/introduction.shtml</a>) transcriptomes, based on a <em>de novo</em> assembly using default settings in Trinity (<a id="xref-ref-41-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Haas <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>; File S5). Sequences were aligned with LASTZ (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Harris 2007</a>). Effective codon usage was calculated using ENCprime (<a href="https://jnpopgen.org/software/">https://jnpopgen.org/software/</a>).</p><p id="p-26">To estimate the rate of Y-sequence evolution, we identified one-to-one orthologs between the <em>M. annua</em> sex linked genes, and the closely related species <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R. communis</em>, through reciprocal best BLASTP, with an <em>e</em>-value of 1e<sup>−50</sup>, a culling limit of 1, and considering only transcripts that were not split across contigs. The 98 orthologs identified were aligned with LASTZ (<a id="xref-ref-42-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Harris 2007</a>) and analyzed with PAML 4.9 (<a id="xref-ref-130-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-130">Yang 2007</a>) to obtain tree-based estimates of d<sub>N</sub> and d<sub>S</sub> values. We employed three models: the “null model” (M<sub>0</sub>), where all parts of the tree are assumed to have the same d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> values; the “branch-specific” model (M<sub>1</sub>), where each branch may have its own d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> value; and the “two-ratio” model (M<sub>2</sub>), where the branch of interest (Y) may have a d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> ratio that differs from the rest of the tree (<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Nielsen and Yang 1998</a>). Preliminary analysis revealed similar results for the M<sub>1</sub> and M<sub>2</sub> models (results not shown); we thus chose M<sub>2</sub> as the simpler model for further analysis. We tested whether the Y branch evolved differently from the rest of the tree, using a likelihood ratio test (LRT) between the M<sub>2</sub> and M<sub>0</sub> models, with one degree of freedom and LRT = 2 × abs(l<sub>2</sub>−l<sub>0</sub>), where l<sub>2</sub> and l<sub>0</sub> are the likelihoods for M<sub>2</sub> and M<sub>0</sub>, respectively.</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Expression analysis</h3><p id="p-27">Reads from the RNA libraries of all 30 females and 35 males were pseudoaligned to the reference transcriptome using Kallisto v0.43.1 (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Bray <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>). The resulting raw count data were compiled in a table, and differential expression analysis between male and female samples was conducted with edgeR v3.18.1 (<a id="xref-ref-101-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-101">Robinson <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>), running in R v.3.4.0 (<a id="xref-ref-98-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-98">R Development Core Team 2007</a>). Genes were filtered from the analysis if their average log<sub>2</sub> count per million (as computed by edgeR’s aveLogCPM function) was negative. This retained genes with an average count of ≥24 per sample. Two individual libraries were removed that were obvious outliers in the MDS plots of the filtered data. Libraries were normalized with the default (TMM) normalization. Dispersion was measured with default parameters using a negative binomial model. Sex-biased transcripts were defined statistically, <em>i.e.</em>, by a false discovery rate (FDR; <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Benjamini and Hochberg 1995</a>) of 5%. A statistical definition for sex-biased genes is justified because our RNAseq analysis was based on many samples, and because the sex-biased categories (male-, female-, and unbiased) used in our analysis capture biological information in that they differed statistically for various measurements. Results for effects of sex bias should be conservative, because our approach diluted “real” sex-biased genes with unbiased genes. We note, however, that the lack of minimum log<sub>2</sub>FC in our definition of sex-bias means that we cannot differentiate between allometric differences in tissue composition or gene regulation differences between males and females (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Montgomery and Mank 2016</a>).</p><p id="p-28">SEX-DETector calculates X- and Y-allele expression from each male. X and Y read numbers were summed for each contig and individual separately, divided by the number of X/Y SNPs of the contig, and adjusted for the library size of the respective individual. X and Y expression levels were then averaged among individuals, and the ratio of the means was computed. Gene ontology (GO) enrichment analysis was performed on the sex-biased genes, as well as separately for the male- and female-biased genes, using topGO with the weight01 algorithm, which accounts for GO topology (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Alexa and Rahnenfuhrer 2010</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Comparison of expression between sex-linked genes and autosomal genes</h3><p id="p-29">We categorized transcripts based on their genomic location into fully sex-linked (SL; transcripts in the nonrecombining region of LG1 in males), pseudoautosomal (PAR; transcripts in the rest of LG1 that showed recombination in both sexes), and autosomal (Au; transcripts that mapped to the remaining seven linkage groups). We investigated the effects of sex linkage, sex-biased expression, and their interactions, through linear models on the following metrics, using R version 3.4.2: absolute male/female log<sub>2</sub>FC (fold-change, <em>i.e.</em>, overall sex-bias intensity); nucleotide diversity (<em>π</em>); d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> from pairwise comparisons with <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R. communis</em>; and Y/X allele-specific log<sub>2</sub>FC obtained from SEX-DETector. The data were not normally distributed, and were thus Box-Cox-transformed after adding 1e<sup>−11</sup> to all values to allow inclusion of zero values and estimating the transformation parameter using the command boxcoxnc with the Anderson-Darling method in the R package AID v2.3 (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Dag <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>). Absolute male/female (log<sub>2</sub>FC) and <em>π</em> remained non-normal after transformation when unbiased genes were included in the analysis, because there were too many transcripts with ∼0 values. Exclusion of these transcripts did not qualitatively affect the results (data not shown). We also analyzed the sex-biased genes on their own within the same statistical framework. This allowed a more similar distribution between the sex-bias categories, and successful tests of normality after Box-Cox transformation. The summary tables from the models were generated using the R package sjPlot version 2.4 (<a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Lüdecke 2017</a>).</p><p id="p-30">We also compared the proportions of sex-biased/unbiased genes and male-biased/female-biased genes between pairs of genomic regions of differing sex linkage using chi square tests (or Fishers’s exact tests when the gene numbers were small), employing the sjt.xtab function of the in R package sjPlot v 2.4 (<a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Lüdecke 2017</a>). The pairs of genomic regions compared were: PAR-SL, Au-LG1, and Au-SL. Finally, we looked for enrichment of candidate genes in the same genomic region comparisons using the same methodology.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Divergence between males and females from natural populations</h3><p id="p-31">To assess divergence between males and females, we examined <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> and sex-biased heterozygosity from individuals sampled from across the species’ range. Seeds obtained from multiple <em>M. annua</em> populations (Table S2) were germinated and grown in a glasshouse at the University of Lausanne. Twenty individuals of each sex were selected for genomic analysis to achieve a balance between representation of many populations and sufficient common sequences of good quality. High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from ∼100 mg of frozen leaf material using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen), following standard protocols. Based on the successful probes from a previous gene-capture experiment (see <a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">González-Martínez <em>et al.</em> 2017</a>), ∼21 Mbp of the <em>M. annua</em> diploid genome (175,000 120-mer probes) were sequenced, with sequence capture using SureSelect DNA Capture technology (Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA) followed by sequencing using Illumina HiSeq 2500, outsourced to Rapid Genomics (Gainesville, FL). Our gene-capture experiment included both genic and intergenic regions and avoided repetitive regions and organelle genomes. Raw data were provided by Rapid Genomics as FASTQ files.</p><p id="p-32">Sequencing quality was assessed using FASTQC v.10.5 (<a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc">www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc</a>). Adapter removal and trimming used Trimmomatic v.0.36 (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Bolger <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>), with options ILLUMINACLIP: TruSeq3-PE.fa:2:30:10 LEADING:3 TRAILING:3 SLIDINGWINDOW: 4:15 MINLEN:36. After filtering, we retained an average of 13 million paired-end sequences per sample. We aligned sequences to the <em>M. annua</em> scaffolds using BWA-MEM v.0.7.12 (<a id="xref-ref-63-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Li and Durbin 2009</a>) and processed the alignments with Picard Tools v.1.141 (<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>). We ran Samtools mpileup (<a id="xref-ref-64-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Li <em>et al.</em> 2009</a>) with the probabilistic alignment disabled, and called SNPs using Varscan (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Koboldt <em>et al.</em> 2009</a>), with a minimum variant allele frequency of 0.15, and a minimum threshold for homozygotes of 0.85. We required a minimum of 10 reads per site, and a Phred quality score &gt;20.</p><p id="p-33">The gene capture ORFs were aligned to the genomic scaffolds using BLAST v.2.2.31 (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Altschul <em>et al.</em> 1990</a>). We retained hits with a maximum <em>e</em>-value of e<sup>−20</sup>, and a minimum identity of 95%. In the case of multiple ORFs mapping to the same genomic location, we selected the longest alignment, and removed all overlapping ORFs. This resulted in 21,203 transcripts aligning to the genome, 8317 of which were also included in the linkage map. All scaffolds with a transcript were assigned a linkage group position in the map. A total of 31 scaffolds contained transcripts assigned to more than one linkage group; in these cases, we assigned the scaffold to the linkage group with &gt;50% of the transcripts (in the case of equal assignment, we excluded the scaffold from the analysis). In total, we were able to assign 6458 scaffolds to linkage-group positions.</p><p id="p-34">We used the BLAST output to construct a bed file that defined the coordinates of each transcript on a genomic scaffold. We then extracted genetic information for the transcripts specified in the bed file. Using this information, we calculated two complementary measures of differentiation between males and females for transcripts &gt;100 bp. First, we computed <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> for each transcript using VCFtools v.0.1.15 (<a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Danecek <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>). Similarly, we assessed sex-biased heterozygosity for each transcript (<a id="xref-ref-115-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-115">Toups <em>et al.</em> 2019</a>), which we defined as the log<sub>10</sub> of the male heterozygosity:female heterozygosity, where heterozygosity is measured as the fraction of sites that are heterozygous. We expect this ratio to be zero for autosomal transcripts, and elevated on young sex chromosomes due to excess heterozygosity in males. For transcripts on older sex chromosomes, the X and Y alleles may have diverged sufficiently to prevent Y alleles from mapping to the X allele reference; in this case, we should expect X-chromosome hemizygosity in males and no inflated female heterozygosity.</p><p id="p-35">For both <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> and sex-biased heterozygosity in the two sexes, we assessed differences between the autosomes, PAR, and the sex-linked region using Wilcoxon signed rank tests. We then computed moving averages in sliding windows of 20 transcripts on all chromosomes using the rollmean function, from package zoo v.1.8-0 in R v3.2.2. To identify regions of elevated <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> and sex-biased heterozygosity, we computed 95% confidence intervals on the basis of an average of 30 consecutive transcripts on the autosomes calculated 1000 times. Finally, for both metrics, we assessed whether the top 5% of transcripts were overrepresented on LG1 using chi-squared tests.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Data availability</h3><p id="p-36">All raw DNA and RNA sequence data generated in this study have been submitted to NCBI under accession SRP098613 BioProject ID PRJNA369310. Scripts, supplemental files, and intermediate files are available at <a href="https://osf.io/a9wjb/">https://osf.io/a9wjb/</a>. File S1 contains the genetic map data and other information on each mapped transcript. File S2 contains the Sanger sequence of clones containing the X and Y variants, where there is a premature stop codon on the Y, and the script used to make the phylogenetic tree. File S3 summarizes the GO results for sex-biased genes. File S4 contains transcriptome annotation information. File S5 contains the assembled transcriptome and X and Y haplotype predictions from SEX-DETector. File S6 contains the genome and predicted genes on it. File S7 is the <em>M. annua</em> repeat library.</p></div></div><div class="section results" id="sec-13"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Male karyotype</h3><p id="p-37"><em>The M. annua</em> karyotype has eight linkage groups, with no difference between males and females, <em>i.e.</em>, the sex chromosomes are homomorphic (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1</a> and Figure S1). Idiogram data, chromosomal arm ratios, and relative length measurements from 50 images (obtained from five individuals; Table S3) indicate that all <em>M. annua</em> chromosomes can be discriminated from one another. A partial distinction between the one large, two medium, and five smaller chromosomes is possible on the basis of the two common ribosomal DNA probes (45S and 5S), with a secondary constriction often visible at the location of the 45S locus.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Basic karyotype for a M.&#xA0;annua male individual. The top row shows DAPI counterstain. The middle row shows chromosomes counterstained with DAPI (blue) after bicolor FISH with 45S rDNA (red) and 5S rDNA (green). The bottom row shows an idiogram of the male chromosomes. The white bar in the middle row represents 5&#xA0;&#xB5;m." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Basic karyotype for a &lt;em&gt;M.&#xA0;annua&lt;/em&gt; male individual. The top row shows DAPI counterstain. The middle row shows chromosomes counterstained with DAPI (blue) after bicolor FISH with 45S rDNA (red) and 5S rDNA (green). The bottom row shows an idiogram of the male chromosomes. The white bar in the middle row represents 5&#xA0;&#xB5;m.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 1" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="251"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 1" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="251"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445856" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1</span> <p id="p-38" class="first-child">Basic karyotype for a <em>M. annua</em> male individual. The top row shows DAPI counterstain. The middle row shows chromosomes counterstained with DAPI (blue) after bicolor FISH with 45S rDNA (red) and 5S rDNA (green). The bottom row shows an idiogram of the male chromosomes. The white bar in the middle row represents 5 µm.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3><em>M. annua</em> genome assembly</h3><p id="p-39">Using the k-mer distribution, we estimate the diploid genome size to be 639 Mb (Figure S3), which is consistent with the previously published results from flow cytometry (<a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Obbard <em>et al.</em> 2006</a>). Using a combination of short-read Illumina and long-read Pacific Biosciences DNA sequencing, we generated ∼56.3 Gb of sequence data from the <em>M. annua</em> male M1, corresponding to a coverage of ∼86.6×. After read filtering, genome coverage dropped to ∼63.7× (Table S5). <em>De novo</em> assembly and scaffolding yielded a final assembly of 89% of the genome (78% without gaps), 65% of which was assembled in scaffolds &gt;1 kb, with an N<sub>50</sub> of 12,808 across 74,927 scaffolds (Table S6). Assembly statistics were consistent with those for other species of the Malpighiales (Table S7), as was our estimate of total genomic GC content (34.7%; <a id="xref-ref-108-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-108">Smarda <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). The <em>M. annua</em> assembly encompassed over 89% of the assembled transcriptome; most of the unassembled genome sequence data are therefore likely repetitive. Using BUSCO2 (<a id="xref-ref-107-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-107">Simão <em>et al.</em> 2015</a>), we recovered 76.1% of the 1440 genes in the BUSCO embryophyte database (of which 3.9% were duplicated). A total of 10.3% of the genes were fragmented, and 13.6% were missing.</p><p id="p-40">Repeat masking identified simple tandem repeats in &gt;10% of the assembly. This proportion is likely to be an underestimate, given the difficulty in assembling microsatellites. We characterized 15% of the assembly on the basis of homology information and DNA transposon and retrotransposon masking, with an additional 33% that corresponds to 1472 predicted novel transposable elements. The most frequent transposable repeats were <em>gypsy</em> LTR, <em>copia</em> LTR, and L1 LINE retrotransposons (Table S8), similar to findings for other plant genomes (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Chan <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-104-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">Sato <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-120-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-120">J. Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-95-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">Rahman <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). Across all data, 58% of the ungapped <em>M. annua</em> assembly was repetitive (Table S8), corresponding to 44% of the genome. Given that our assembly covered 78% of the genome, and if we assume that the missing fraction comprises only repeats, up to 66% of the <em>M. annua</em> genome may be repetitive. This too would be consistent with what is known for other plants, which have a similarly high AT-rich repeat content and a similar number of unclassified repetitive elements (<em>e.g.</em>, <a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Chan <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>). The <em>M. annua</em> genome appears to be ∼240 Mb larger than that of its relative <em>R. communis</em> (see Table S7), perhaps reflecting ongoing transposon activity.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Sex-linked transcripts and genetic map for diploid <em>M. annua</em></h3><p id="p-41">Genetic mapping recovered eight linkage groups (LG1 through LG8), corresponding to the expected chromosome number for the diploid <em>M. annua</em> karyotype (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Durand 1963</a>). LepMap3 (LM3) identified 568 sex-linked (SL) transcripts, of which 365 were also identified as sex-linked by SEX-DETector, and phased into X and Y haplotypes. The 568 SL transcripts mapped to LG1, which is thus the sex chromosome. An additional 1209 transcripts mapped to the two ends of LG1, representing two putative pseudoautosomal regions (PAR1 + PAR2; we refer to these as PAR and consider them together in all analyses); 8299 transcripts mapped to the other (autosomal) linkage groups. In total, 641 markers could be resolved from one another and were thus informative. We inferred the sex-linked region to comprise transcripts without recombination in males and in the same phase as that of their paternal grandfather. Assuming an equal transcript density across the genome, the sex-linked region represents 32.6% (about a third) of LG1, and 5.8% of the 320 Mb haploid genome (<a id="xref-ref-81-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Obbard <em>et al.</em> 2006</a>), or 18.6 Mb. This Y-linked region maps to LG1 at male position 53.85 cM and spans 22.23 cM on the female recombination map (from positions 46.44 to 68.67 cM; <a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a>). The total male and female recombination maps were 700.43 and 716.06 cM, respectively. All marker names and their associated positions and metrics are provided in File S1.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genetic map of the eight linkage groups (LG) for M. annua, with each LG represented by the male (left) and female (right) maps. Lines linking the two maps indicate the mapping position of groups of transcripts that segregated together (10,076 total transcripts). The sex-linked region is at 53.85&#xA0;cM of chromosome 1 (LG1) on the male recombination map, and is highlighted by the gray vertical bar. The two pseudoautosomal regions (PAR) comprise the rest of LG1." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Genetic map of the eight linkage groups (LG) for &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;, with each LG represented by the male (left) and female (right) maps. Lines linking the two maps indicate the mapping position of groups of transcripts that segregated together (10,076 total transcripts). The sex-linked region is at 53.85&#xA0;cM of chromosome 1 (LG1) on the male recombination map, and is highlighted by the gray vertical bar. The two pseudoautosomal regions (PAR) comprise the rest of LG1.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 2" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="292"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 2" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="292"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 2" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445833" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 2</span> <p id="p-42" class="first-child">Genetic map of the eight linkage groups (LG) for <em>M. annua</em>, with each LG represented by the male (left) and female (right) maps. Lines linking the two maps indicate the mapping position of groups of transcripts that segregated together (10,076 total transcripts). The sex-linked region is at 53.85 cM of chromosome 1 (LG1) on the male recombination map, and is highlighted by the gray vertical bar. The two pseudoautosomal regions (PAR) comprise the rest of LG1.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Characterization of contigs by sex linkage and ORF localization</h3><p id="p-43">We characterized the genome assembly by mapping all ORFs using GMAP and dividing genomic contigs into three groups: “sex-linked,” “autosomal,” and “expressed ORF” contigs (containing all contigs that mapped to a transcript, regardless of its inclusion on the genetic map; note that PAR contigs were included in the autosomal bin). There were 548 sex-linked contigs containing 1641 genes and a total of 8.3 Mb of sequence; 7579 autosomal contigs containing 23,502 genes, and 106 Mb sequence data; and 15,392 contigs with 34,105 expressed ORFs distributed across 176 Mb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/445935/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/445935/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1</span> <span class="caption-title">Summary statistics of contigs containing sex-linked, autosomal or all expressed ORFs</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Sequence divergence, nucleotide diversity, and codon usage of X and Y haplotypes</h3><p id="p-45">We used the SNP and haplotype calls from SEX-DETector to compare nucleotide diversity and divergence between the X and Y ORFs within <em>M. annua</em>, as well as between X, Y, and autosomal ORFs of <em>M. annua</em> and closely related species. The mean pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> between X and Y haplotypes within <em>M</em>. annua was 0.182 (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). For sex-linked transcripts, we excluded the few SNPs that did not show clear sex-linked segregation, but we could not apply such error correction for autosomes. Accordingly, <em>π</em> might be underestimated for sex-linked genes compared to autosomes (see Figure S3 and <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>), so that metrics associated with sex-linked sequences should be regarded as conservative.</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/445905/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/445905/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2</span> <span class="caption-title">Average pairwise d<sub>S</sub> and d<sub>N</sub> for ORFs of <em>M. annua</em> from X/Y haplotype comparison within <em>M. annua</em> or against <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R. communis</em> sequences</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-47">The pairwise d<sub>S</sub> and d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> values for all possible orthologs between <em>M. annua</em> and its relatives <em>M. huetti</em> and <em>R. communis</em>, and the X and Y haplotypes, are presented in <a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>. For autosomal genes, d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> between <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> and between <em>M. annua</em> and <em>R. communis</em>, respectively, was 0.149 and 0.140. Pairwise d<sub>S</sub> was lower between X/Y gene pairs within <em>M. annua</em> than between orthologous autosomal genes in <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> or <em>R. communis</em>.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Density plots of synonymous site divergence (dS) for: (A) all M. annua X/Y haplotypes, (B) autosomal M. annua/M. huetii homologs, (C) autosomal M. annua/R. communis homologs, (D) X-linked M. annua/M. huetii homologs, and (E) X-linked M. annua/R. communis homologs. The numbers on the bottom right indicate the sample size for each plot." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Density plots of synonymous site divergence (d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;) for: (A) all &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt; X/Y haplotypes, (B) autosomal &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt; homologs, (C) autosomal &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;R. communis&lt;/em&gt; homologs, (D) X-linked &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt; homologs, and (E) X-linked &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;R. communis&lt;/em&gt; homologs. The numbers on the bottom right indicate the sample size for each plot.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="439"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="439"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445903" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3</span> <p id="p-48" class="first-child">Density plots of synonymous site divergence (d<sub>S</sub>) for: (A) all <em>M. annua</em> X/Y haplotypes, (B) autosomal <em>M. annua</em>/<em>M. huetii</em> homologs, (C) autosomal <em>M. annua</em>/<em>R. communis</em> homologs, (D) X-linked <em>M. annua</em>/<em>M. huetii</em> homologs, and (E) X-linked <em>M. annua</em>/<em>R. communis</em> homologs. The numbers on the bottom right indicate the sample size for each plot.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-49">Codon usage in <em>M. annua</em> did not differ significantly between sex-linked, pseudoautosomal and autosomal (SL, PAR, and Au) ORFs (Nc = 54, Nc = 53, and Nc = 53, respectively; Figure S4).</p><p id="p-50">We used PAML (<a id="xref-ref-130-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-130">Yang 2007</a>) to analyze the 98 sex-linked genes with orthologs in <em>M. annua</em>, <em>M. huetii</em>, and <em>R. communis</em>, using the “two-ratio” M<sub>2</sub> model (which accommodates differences in d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> between the Y branch and other branches of the tree; <a id="xref-ref-80-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Nielsen and Yang 1998</a>). Our analysis identified 84 sequences with 0.001 &lt; d<sub>S</sub> &lt; 2 on both X and Y branches.
