Variation for 74 of these sequences was consistent with a tree topology corresponding to the species tree and recent X-Y divergence for <em>M. annua</em>, <em>i.e.</em>, trees with topology {[(<em>M. annua</em>-X, <em>M. annua</em>-Y), <em>M. huetii</em>], <em>R. communis</em>}; we focused further analysis on these genes. For 52 of these 74 genes (21 after FDR correction at 5% level; <a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Benjamini and Hochberg 1995</a>), the M<sub>2</sub> model provided a better fit than the M<sub>0</sub> model. Moreover, the Y lineage had more synonymous and more nonsynonymous mutations compared to the X lineage following divergence from <em>M. huetii</em>. Together, these results suggest that 28% of the Y-linked genes in our sample have been evolving faster than their corresponding X-linked orthologs, as inferred from the tree-based d<sub>N</sub> and d<sub>S</sub> values (Wilcoxon Rank Sum = 392 and 568, respectively; <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001 for both tests; <a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>), as well as under relaxed selection, as inferred from the tree-based d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> ratio (Wilcoxon Rank Sum = 267, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001; <a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>). The seven sequences for which the Y variant clustered outside the clade (<em>M. annua</em>-X, <em>M. huetii</em>) were scattered across the genetic map of the nonrecombining region of the Y (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>), <em>i.e.</em>, they did not indicate a clear ancestral/common sex-determining region between <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em>. We did not investigate these genes further.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Boxplots of tree-based estimates of dN/dS, dS, and dN for the X, Y, M. huetii, and R. communis branches of the 72 gene trees that correspond to tree topology {[(M. annua-X, M. annua-Y), M. huetii], R. communis}. A Y-sequence dN/dS outlier of 2.7 and an M. huetii-sequence dS outlier of 0.23 are not shown." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Boxplots of tree-based estimates of d&lt;sub&gt;N&lt;/sub&gt;/d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;, d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;, and d&lt;sub&gt;N&lt;/sub&gt; for the X, Y, &lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;R. communis&lt;/em&gt; branches of the 72 gene trees that correspond to tree topology {[(&lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;-X, &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;-Y), &lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt;], &lt;em&gt;R. communis&lt;/em&gt;}. A Y-sequence d&lt;sub&gt;N&lt;/sub&gt;/d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt; outlier of 2.7 and an &lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt;-sequence d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt; outlier of 0.23 are not shown.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 4" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="119"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 4" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="119"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 4" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445943" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 4</span> <p id="p-51" class="first-child">Boxplots of tree-based estimates of d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub>, d<sub>S</sub>, and d<sub>N</sub> for the X, Y, <em>M. huetii</em>, and <em>R. communis</em> branches of the 72 gene trees that correspond to tree topology {[(<em>M. annua</em>-X, <em>M. annua</em>-Y), <em>M. huetii</em>], <em>R. communis</em>}. A Y-sequence d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> outlier of 2.7 and an <em>M. huetii</em>-sequence d<sub>S</sub> outlier of 0.23 are not shown.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Summary of transcript metrics calculated for sequences across the female recombination map of the putative sex chromosome LG1, based on analysis of X and Y sequences combined. The nonrecombining region in males is located between the vertical dotted lines. Orange points in the top panel indicate significant sex-bias (5% FDR). Potential candidate sex-determining genes are indicated by letters, with their putative function given in the inset legend. The only verified premature stop codon on the Y copy is indicated by ks beside the nonrecombining region (i.e., for a kelch protein gene). Transcripts for which the Y sequence diverged prior to the M. annua/M. huetii species split are labeled &#x201C;2.&#x201D;" class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Summary of transcript metrics calculated for sequences across the female recombination map of the putative sex chromosome LG1, based on analysis of X and Y sequences combined. The nonrecombining region in males is located between the vertical dotted lines. Orange points in the top panel indicate significant sex-bias (5% FDR). Potential candidate sex-determining genes are indicated by letters, with their putative function given in the inset legend. The only verified premature stop codon on the Y copy is indicated by k&lt;sub&gt;s&lt;/sub&gt; beside the nonrecombining region (&lt;em&gt;i.e.&lt;/em&gt;, for a kelch protein gene). Transcripts for which the Y sequence diverged prior to the &lt;em&gt;M. annua&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt; species split are labeled &#x201C;2.&#x201D;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F5.medium.gif" width="373" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 5" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F5.medium.gif" width="373" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 5" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445861" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5</span> <p id="p-52" class="first-child">Summary of transcript metrics calculated for sequences across the female recombination map of the putative sex chromosome LG1, based on analysis of X and Y sequences combined. The nonrecombining region in males is located between the vertical dotted lines. Orange points in the top panel indicate significant sex-bias (5% FDR). Potential candidate sex-determining genes are indicated by letters, with their putative function given in the inset legend. The only verified premature stop codon on the Y copy is indicated by k<sub>s</sub> beside the nonrecombining region (<em>i.e.</em>, for a kelch protein gene). Transcripts for which the Y sequence diverged prior to the <em>M. annua</em>/<em>M. huetii</em> species split are labeled “2.”</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Identification of a degenerated and duplicated sequence on the Y</h3><p id="p-53">The SEX-DETector pipeline detected putative premature stop codons in four Y and three X transcripts, although there was always a functional X haplotype for the same transcript. We confirmed one of the four Y-haplotype stop codons using PCR, through cloning and sequencing from five males and nine females sampled across the geographic range of diploid <em>M. annua</em>, which only showed the stop codons on sequences from males. Briefly, a phylogenetic tree was constructed for this gene (Figure S5) using RAxML v.8.2.10 using 1000 bootstraps (<a id="xref-ref-112-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-112">Stamatakis 2014</a>) on sequences aligned with mafft v7.310 (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Katoh and Standley 2013</a>). Interestingly, two different Y-linked sequences could be obtained, sometimes from the same male, suggesting that the sequence has been duplicated in at least some males. All males and females analyzed also contained a functional copy that had 89% identity with an F-box/kelch repeat protein in <em>R. communis</em>. The transcript shows a lower Y/X expression ratio and a high <em>π</em> (marked “K<sub>s</sub>” in <a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>), suggesting that it represents a degenerated copy on the Y.</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Identification and annotation of sex-biased genes</h3><p id="p-54">There were many more (1385) male-biased than female-biased genes (325), based on 5% FDR. This difference increased when bias was determined on the basis of a minimum log<sub>2</sub> fold-change (log<sub>2</sub>FC) threshold of 1 (1141 <em>vs.</em> 140; Figure S6). Male-biased genes were enriched in biological functions such as anther-wall tapetum development, response to auxin, response to ethylene, cell-tip growth, pollen-tube growth, and floral-organ senescence, whereas female-biased genes were enriched for functions related to the maintenance of inflorescence meristem identity, jasmonic acid and ethylene-dependent systemic resistance, regulation of innate immune response, and seed maturation (File S3).</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Identification of the ancestral evolutionary stratum on the Y</h3><p id="p-55">To identify a putative ancestral stratum in the nonrecombining region, we investigated variation in the following metrics across the female recombination map (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>): the magnitude of sex-biased expression; the nucleotide diversity (<em>π</em>) of each transcript; and the pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> ratio based on comparison with homologous genes in the closely related dioecious sister species <em>M. huetti</em>, and its monoecious more distant relative <em>R. communis</em> (for which we could identify 4761 and 2993 homologs, respectively); and the expression ratio of X and Y haplotypes inferred from SEX-DETector (<a id="xref-ref-78-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Muyle <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>). We annotated <a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a> with letters to indicate the position of candidate sex-determining genes, based on their blast hit descriptions. The sole obvious outlier for any of the metrics above was a transcript associated with auxin production, which had a high pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> value in the comparison with the close outgroup species, <em>M. huetii</em>.</p></div><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h3>Comparison of sex-linked, autosomal, and pseudoautosomal ORFs</h3><p id="p-56">We examined the effects of sex-bias (male-, female-, and unbiased genes) and genomic location (SL, PAR, or Au), as well as their interactions, on the five metrics plotted in <a id="xref-fig-5-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>. We repeated the analyses after excluding unbiased transcripts for the four metrics with sufficient gene numbers (sex bias log<sub>2</sub>FC, <em>π</em>, and pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> using either <em>M. huetii</em> or <em>R. communis</em>) to directly compare male- and female-biased transcripts. The full models are presented in Tables S9–S11. Below, we summarize their significant results; <a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 6</a> shows the data distributions. The analyses revealed significantly higher differences in gene expression between the sexes (absolute log<sub>2</sub>FC) in the sex-linked region (<em>P</em> = 0.037; Table S9). These differences were largely due to significantly higher expression of male- than female-biased transcripts(male-bias*SL interaction: <em>P</em> = 0.036; Table S10).</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Graphical summary of the analysis of genes based on their sex-bias (blue: male-biased, red: female-biased, gray: unbiased) and sex linkage (SL: sex linked, PAR: remaining LG1, Au: LG 2-7). (A) absolute log2FC expression difference between the sexes, (B) nucleotide diversity (&#x3C0;), (C) pairwise dN/dS compared to M. huetii homologs, (D) pairwise dN/dS compared to R. communis homologs, (E) relative expression of Y over X alleles. The numbers to the right indicate the number of genes in each category. Data are plotted prior to Box-Cox transformation." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Graphical summary of the analysis of genes based on their sex-bias (blue: male-biased, red: female-biased, gray: unbiased) and sex linkage (SL: sex linked, PAR: remaining LG1, Au: LG 2-7). (A) absolute log&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FC expression difference between the sexes, (B) nucleotide diversity (&lt;em&gt;&#x3C0;&lt;/em&gt;), (C) pairwise d&lt;sub&gt;N&lt;/sub&gt;/d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt; compared to &lt;em&gt;M. huetii&lt;/em&gt; homologs, (D) pairwise d&lt;sub&gt;N&lt;/sub&gt;/d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt; compared to &lt;em&gt;R. communis&lt;/em&gt; homologs, (E) relative expression of Y over X alleles. The numbers to the right indicate the number of genes in each category. Data are plotted prior to Box-Cox transformation.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 6" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F6.medium.gif" width="305" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 6" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F6.medium.gif" width="305" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 6" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445910" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 6</span> <p id="p-57" class="first-child">Graphical summary of the analysis of genes based on their sex-bias (blue: male-biased, red: female-biased, gray: unbiased) and sex linkage (SL: sex linked, PAR: remaining LG1, Au: LG 2-7). (A) absolute log<sub>2</sub>FC expression difference between the sexes, (B) nucleotide diversity (<em>π</em>), (C) pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> compared to <em>M. huetii</em> homologs, (D) pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> compared to <em>R. communis</em> homologs, (E) relative expression of Y over X alleles. The numbers to the right indicate the number of genes in each category. Data are plotted prior to Box-Cox transformation.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-58">The increased magnitude of male-biased gene expression in fully sex-linked genes is consistent with a recent masculinization as sex-linked genes had slightly lower nucleotide diversity (<em>π</em>) than those in the PAR or autosomal genes (<em>P</em> = 0.039; Table S9), but male-biased genes were not affected as much (<em>P</em> = 0.017; Table S9). The pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> between <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> was higher for sex-linked genes (<em>P</em> = 0.017; Table S9), as well as for female-biased genes localizing to the PAR (PAR*female-bias interaction: <em>P</em> = 0.005; Table S9). Sex-biased genes in the <em>M. annua</em> PAR had a lower <em>π</em> (PAR: <em>P</em> = 0.007; Table S10), and the d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> for sex-biased PAR genes between the <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> was also lower (<em>P</em> = 0.018; Table S10). d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> with respect to the more distantly related <em>R. communis</em> was only higher for male-biased genes (<em>P</em> = 0.046; Table S9), though power was limited by the availability of fewer identified homologs (4761 in <em>M. huetti</em> compared to 2993 in <em>R. communis</em>). The Y-linked copy of female-biased genes had significantly lower expression than its X-linked counterpart (<em>P</em> = 0.035; Table S11).</p><p id="p-59">Finally, we used chi-squared tests to compare the relative proportions of sex-biased <em>vs.</em> unbiased and male- <em>vs.</em> female-biased genes in different pairs of genomic regions (PAR-SL, Au-LG1, Au-SL; <a id="xref-table-wrap-3-1" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>). The sex-linked region had significantly more sex-biased genes (compared to either PAR or Au regions). This enrichment was largely responsible for the significant sex-biased gene enrichment compared with the entire LG1 and the autosomes, and was largely due to more female-biased genes on LG1. The sex chromosomes thus appear to be feminized in terms of number of female-biased genes, in contrast to the genome as a whole, which was masculinized in terms of number of male-biased gene numbers, and, in contrast to absolute expression, which was higher for male-biased genes. We did not detect any enrichment for candidate genes with functions with obvious potential sexually antagonistic effects in the sex-linked region (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-2" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>).</p><div id="T3" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/445811/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/445811/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 3</span> <span class="caption-title">Results of chi-squared tests for differences in the frequencies of sex-biased <em>vs.</em> -unbiased genes, male- <em>vs.</em> female-biased genes, and candidate sex-determining genes (CG) <em>vs.</em> other genes (not CG) between the pseudoautosomal region (PAR), the sex-linked region (SL), linkage group 1 (LG1), and autosomes (Au)</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><h3>Sex-linked sequence analysis of individuals sampled across the species range</h3><p id="p-61">To confirm the lack of recombination in the sex-linked region, we estimated <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> and sex-biased heterozygosity for genome capture data from 20 males and 20 females sampled from natural populations across the species’ range (Table S2), based on 6557 transcripts assigned to the linkage map. The sex-linked region showed clear differentiation between males and females using both metrics. <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> between males and females was elevated in the sex-linked region relative to the PAR and the autosomes (Wilcoxon, <em>P</em> = 4.79 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, <em>P</em> = 1.099 × 10<sup>−7</sup>, respectively; <a id="xref-fig-7-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7A</a>), although the <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> values were very small. Similarly, males were more heterozygous than females in the sex-linked region relative to the PAR and the autosomes (Wilcoxon, <em>P</em> &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup> in both; <a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7C</a>). A sliding window analyses of <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> and sex-biased heterozygosity identified a region of elevated <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> at ∼65 cM of LG1 in the female recombination map (<a id="xref-fig-7-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7B</a>)—a value exceeding that for any autosomal region. We also identified a region between 50 and 65 cM on LG1 (<a id="xref-fig-7-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7D</a>) with higher heterozygosity in males than females (<a id="xref-fig-7-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7E</a>). Finally, both <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> and male-biased heterozygosity were greatest in the sex-linked region of LG1 (Table S12).</p><div id="F7" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F7.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Summary of the effect of sex linkage on SNP metrics from genome capture data across the species range. (A) FST for sex linkage category, (B) distribution of FST across the female recombination map for LG1, (C) ratio of male/female heterozygosity for sex linkage category, (D) ratio of male/female heterozygosity across the female recombination map of LG1, (E) observed heterozygosity in males (blue) and females (red) across LG1. Asterisks in (A and C) indicate significant Wilcoxon tests (P &lt; 0.001), the sex-linked region is indicated by vertical lines in (B, D, and E)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1837927826" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Summary of the effect of sex linkage on SNP metrics from genome capture data across the species range. (A) &lt;em&gt;F&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;ST&lt;/sub&gt; for sex linkage category, (B) distribution of &lt;em&gt;F&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;ST&lt;/sub&gt; across the female recombination map for LG1, (C) ratio of male/female heterozygosity for sex linkage category, (D) ratio of male/female heterozygosity across the female recombination map of LG1, (E) observed heterozygosity in males (blue) and females (red) across LG1. Asterisks in (A and C) indicate significant Wilcoxon tests (&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.001), the sex-linked region is indicated by vertical lines in (B, D, and E).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 7" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F7.medium.gif" width="440" height="431"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 7" src="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F7.medium.gif" width="440" height="431"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F7.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 7" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.genetics.org/content/genetics/212/3/815/F7.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/445796" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 7</span> <p id="p-62" class="first-child">Summary of the effect of sex linkage on SNP metrics from genome capture data across the species range. (A) <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> for sex linkage category, (B) distribution of <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub> across the female recombination map for LG1, (C) ratio of male/female heterozygosity for sex linkage category, (D) ratio of male/female heterozygosity across the female recombination map of LG1, (E) observed heterozygosity in males (blue) and females (red) across LG1. Asterisks in (A and C) indicate significant Wilcoxon tests (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.001), the sex-linked region is indicated by vertical lines in (B, D, and E).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-24"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><div id="sec-25" class="subsection"><h3>A genome assembly, annotation, and genetic map for dioecious <em>M. annua</em></h3><p id="p-63">Our study provides a draft assembly and annotation of diploid dioecious <em>M. annua</em> (2<em>n</em> = 16). Cytogenetic analysis has confirmed the haploid number of <em>n</em> = 8 chromosomes for diploid <em>M. annua</em> (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Durand 1963</a>), with ∼10,000 ORFs assigned to the corresponding eight linkage groups by genetic mapping. Similar to other plants, ∼two-thirds of the <em>M. annua</em> genome comprises repetitive sequences, mostly <em>gypsy</em> LTR, <em>copia</em> LTR, and L1 LINE retrotransposons (<a id="xref-ref-17-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Chan <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-104-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">Sato <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-120-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-120">J. Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-95-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">Rahman <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). The number of genes in <em>M. annua</em> (34,105) is similar to that of diploid species such as <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (&gt;27,000 genes) and <em>R. communis</em> (&gt;31,000 genes) and is thus compatible with having a long recent history under diploidy rather than polyploidy; for comparison, the gene content of diploid <em>Gossypium</em> is &gt;40,000 genes, pointing to a polyploid history (<a id="xref-ref-120-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-120">J. Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). The draft annotated genome of <em>M. annua</em> will be useful for understanding potential further links between sex determination and sexual dimorphism, as well as the consequences of genome duplication and hybridization (<a id="xref-ref-81-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Obbard <em>et al.</em> 2006</a>) for sexual-system transitions in polyploid lineage of the species complex (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">Pannell <em>et al.</em> 2008</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-26" class="subsection"><h3>The size of the nonrecombining Y-linked region of <em>M. annua</em></h3><p id="p-64">Patterns of SNP segregation in small families from 568 ORFs suggests that a substantial length (one-third) of the <em>M. annua</em> Y chromosome has ceased recombining with the X. Lack of recombination on the sex-linked region is further supported by the existence of a Y-specific nonfunctional gene duplication, which could be amplified in populations ranging from Israel to Britain, as well as additional population-genetic data from males and females sampled from across the diploid species’ range. The sex-linked region had slightly, but significantly, higher <em>F</em><sub>ST</sub>, a higher male/female SNP ratio, and higher heterozygote frequency in males than females, indicating a history of low recombination. Previous mapping of bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) found male-specific PCR products distributed over a region of between 52 and 66.82 cM of the sex-linked region, corresponding to 4.86% of the genome, and implying a physical length of between 14.5 and 19 Mb for the nonrecombining genome (<a id="xref-ref-118-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-118">Veltsos <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>). The large nonrecombining region of the <em>M. annua</em> Y chromosome contrasts with that of other plants with homomorphic sex chromosomes, which typically have &lt;1% of their Y not recombining, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>V. vinifera</em> (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Fechter <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-89-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">Picq <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>), <em>F. chiloensis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-114-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-114">Tennessen <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>), and <em>Populus</em> species (<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Paolucci <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-38-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Geraldes <em>et al.</em> 2015</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-27" class="subsection"><h3>Limited gene loss, pseudogenization, and mild purifying selection on the <em>M. annua</em> Y</h3><p id="p-65">We found only limited evidence for Y-chromosome degeneration within the nonrecombining region of the <em>M. annua</em> Y chromosome, despite its substantial length. Very few genes have been lost or have become nonfunctional, and only one of the 528 X-linked genes corresponding to the nonrecombining region of the Y (0.2%) did not have a Y-linked homolog. Our analysis may have overlooked genes that have been lost from the Y, because their detection from RNAseq data relies on the presence of polymorphisms on the X copy (see <a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Blavet <em>et al.</em> (2015)</a>) and requires their detectable expression in our sampled tissues. Nevertheless, the absence of evidence of substantial gene loss is consistent with other signatures of only mild Y-chromosome degeneration in <em>M. annua</em>, and is likely reliable.</p><p id="p-66">There was only slightly lower relative expression from the Y compared to the X allele. Allele-specific expression was inferred on the basis of mapping to the reference transcriptome, and we expect only limited mapping bias against expressed Y-specific genes. The fact that different levels of Y/X expression depended on sex-bias (it was reduced for female-biased genes) further suggests that the result is probably not an artifact of the analysis. We found some evidence for relaxed purifying selection on Y alleles compared to X alleles, as expected in genomic regions experiencing limited recombination (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">Charlesworth and Charlesworth 2000</a>). Specifically, pairwise comparisons of X and Y alleles (after excluding the few with inframe stop codons) showed higher d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> than pairwise comparisons of <em>M. annua</em> orthologs with <em>M. huetii</em> or <em>R. communis</em>. Moreover, our tree-based analysis of common orthologs in all species indicated that 28% of the identified Y-linked alleles have experienced an accelerated rate of molecular evolution in the <em>M. annua</em> lineage since its divergence from its sister species <em>M. huetii</em>, which is also dioecious. However, we found no evidence for differential codon usage bias between the Y-linked and other sequences. Codon usage bias is expected to be lower for nonrecombining regions of the genome experiencing weaker purifying selection (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Hill and Robertson 1966</a>), and there appears to have been a shift toward less preferred codon usage in <em>R. hastatulus</em> Y-linked genes, increasing in severity with time since the putative cessation of recombination between X and Y chromosomes (<a id="xref-ref-47-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Hough <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>). Although our analysis involves multiple statistical tests of significance, so that some results might represent Type 1 error, taken together they point to a very recent cessation of recombination for much of the Y chromosome. In contrast, up to 28% of <em>R. hastatulus</em> Y-linked genes have been lost (<a id="xref-ref-47-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Hough <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>) and ∼50% of <em>S. latifolia</em> Y-linked genes are dysfunctional (<a id="xref-ref-86-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Papadopulos <em>et al.</em> 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-57-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Krasovec <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-28" class="subsection"><h3>Sex-biased gene expression in <em>M. annua</em></h3><p id="p-67">We found substantial differences in gene expression between males and females of <em>M. annua</em>, consistent with the observation of sexual dimorphism for a number of morphological, life-history, and defense traits in <em>M. annua</em> (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Hesse and Pannell 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Labouche and Pannell 2016</a>). There were at least four times more male-biased than female-biased genes; this is probably conservative because we did not use a minimum log<sub>2</sub>FC threshold in our analysis, which would have further increased the proportion of male-biased genes. The observed slight decrease in expression of Y- compared with X-linked alleles suggests that dosage compensation could have begun evolving. However, any such dosage compensation would likely be at a very early stage, not least because YY males (which lack an X chromosome) are viable (<a id="xref-ref-58-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Kuhn 1939</a>; <a id="xref-ref-131-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-131">Li <em>et</em><em>al.</em> 2019</a>). Interestingly, sex linkage sometimes influenced male- and female-biased genes differently, with male-biased genes having a higher nucleotide diversity (<em>π</em>) than female-biased genes when sex linked, again perhaps reflecting a history of relaxed purifying selection. Male- and female-biased genes also differed in pairwise d<sub>N</sub>/d<sub>S</sub> relative to <em>M. huetii</em>, which was higher for female-biased genes, contrary to expectations under a hypothesis of faster male evolution (<a id="xref-ref-124-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-124">Wu and Davis 1993</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-29" class="subsection"><h3>Evidence for evolutionary strata on the <em>M. annua</em> Y?</h3><p id="p-68">We were unable to find any direct evidence for evolutionary strata with different levels of divergence on the <em>M. annua</em> Y chromosome, but phylogenetic comparisons point to the likely existence of at least two such strata on the <em>M. annua</em> Y. Dioecy evolved in a common ancestor to <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Krähenbühl <em>et al.</em> 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-81-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Obbard <em>et al.</em> 2006</a>), and both species share the same Y chromosome and sex-determining region, as revealed by crosses (<a id="xref-ref-102-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">Russell and Pannell 2015</a>) and the possession of common male-specific PCR markers (<a id="xref-ref-118-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-118">Veltsos <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>). The phylogeny of sex-linked genes within the shared sex-determining region should have the topology [(<em>M. annua</em>-Y, <em>M. huetii</em>), <em>M. annua</em>-X]. In contrast, our PAML analysis clearly indicates that the phylogeny of most genes in the nonrecombining region of the <em>M. annua</em> Y have the topology [(<em>M. annua</em>-Y, <em>M. annua</em>-X), <em>M. huetii</em>], indicating divergence between the X and Y copies of these genes in <em>M. annua</em> more recently than the species split between <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em>. These results suggest that <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> share an old (and possibly small) nonrecombining stratum that includes the sex-determining locus, and that a larger stratum has been added to it in the <em>M. annua</em> lineage. Another possible interpretation is gene conversion between X and Y copies within <em>M. annua</em>, as has been reported for humans and some plants (<a id="xref-ref-97-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">Rautenberg <em>et al.</em> 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-127-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-127">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-116-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-116">Trombetta <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>). This would lead to an underestimate of the size of the ancestral stratum and may have erased evidence of a longer common shared history between the nonrecombining regions of <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em>.</p><p id="p-69">Although most Y-linked genes that we sampled in <em>M. annua</em> point to an X/Y divergence that postdates the species’ split from <em>M. huetii</em>, we found seven genes with a phylogeny consistent with the hypothesized old stratum shared with <em>M. huetii</em>. If these genes are indeed within an older stratum, we might expect them to colocalize in the same part of the nonrecombining region. However, we found that they were quite scattered on the female recombination map. We have no explanation for this pattern, but we note that it would be consistent with a break of synteny between the <em>M. annua</em> Y and X chromosomes (and thus potentially also between the <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>M. annua</em> Y chromosomes) in the region corresponding to the nonrecombining region.</p><p id="p-70">Patterns of divergence in terms of tree-based d<sub>S</sub> and d<sub>N</sub> between the X and Y chromosomes of <em>M. annua</em> and their corresponding orthologs in <em>M. huetii</em> and <em>R. communis</em> were largely consistent with the expected phylogenetic relationships between these species (<a id="xref-ref-56-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Krähenbühl <em>et al.</em> 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-81-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Obbard <em>et al.</em> 2006</a>). The increased d<sub>S</sub> for the Y branch is consistent with a higher mutation rate in male meiosis. Nevertheless, the d<sub>S</sub> branch length for <em>R. communis</em> was lower than expected for a simple molecular clock across the phylogeny. The recent transition to an annual life cycle in the clade that includes <em>M. annua</em> and <em>M. huetii</em> may be partially responsible for the larger number of mutations that have accumulated along their branches compared to the perennial <em>R. communis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-111-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-111">Smith and Donoghue 2008</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-30" class="subsection"><h3>Age of the nonrecombining Y-linked region of <em>M. annua</em></h3><p id="p-71">The age of the <em>M. annua</em> nonrecombining region can be estimated on the basis of inferred mutation rates in plants. <a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">Ossowski <em>et al.</em> (2010)</a> estimated the mutation rate for <em>A. thaliana</em> to be between 7 × 10<sup>−9</sup> and 2.2 × 10<sup>−8</sup> per nucleotide per generation. More recently, <a id="xref-ref-57-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Krasovec <em>et al.</em> (2018)</a> estimated the mutation rate for <em>S. latifolia</em> to be 7.31 × 10<sup>−9</sup>. The similarity of these estimates suggests that they might be broadly applicable. Accepting a mutation rate for plants of ∼7.5 × 10<sup>−9</sup> and using the synonymous site divergence between X- and Y-linked sequences of <em>M. annua</em> of 0.011 (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-3" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>), we infer that recombination between most of the X- and Y-linked genes in <em>M. annua</em> may have ceased as recently as 1.5 million generations ago [more recently if we adopt the upper end of the mutation-rate range estimated by <a id="xref-ref-83-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">Ossowski <em>et al.</em> (2010)</a>]. Given that <em>M. annua</em> is an annual plant, the putative second nonrecombining stratum of the Y chromosome of diploid <em>M. annua</em> could be less than 1 million years old. <a id="xref-ref-57-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Krasovec <em>et al.</em> (2018)</a> used their mutation-rate estimate to infer an age of 11 million years for the oldest sex-linked stratum of <em>S. latifolia</em>, <em>i.e.</em>, an order of magnitude older than the putatively expanded sex-linked region of <em>M. annua</em>. The few estimates for the time since recombination suppression in other plants with homomorphic sex chromosomes, and those with smaller nonrecombining regions, range from 15 to 31.4 MYA (reviewed in <a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Charlesworth 2016</a>). <em>M. annua</em> thus appears to have particularly young sex chromosomes.</p></div><div id="sec-31" class="subsection"><h3>Concluding remarks</h3><p id="p-72">We conclude by speculating on a possible role for sexually antagonistic selection in favoring suppressed recombination on the <em>M. annua</em> Y. The canonical model for sex-chromosome evolution supposes that suppressed recombination originally evolves in response to selection to bring sexually antagonistic loci into linkage with the sex-determining locus, and that the Y chromosome begins to degenerate after recombination has ceased (<a id="xref-ref-100-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">Rice 1987</a>). Given this model, we might expect to see signatures of sexual antagonism in the sex-linked region before the onset of substantial Y-chromosome degeneration. While we do not observe obvious signatures of sexually antagonistic selection, some of the observed patterns in the sex-linked region are difficult to interpret as the outcome of degeneration. For example, while the Y-linked alleles showed slightly lower expression than X-linked alleles, this was not true for male-biased genes. One possible explanation is that the female-biased Y-linked alleles in males might have been driven to lower expression by sexually antagonistic selection. If female-biased expression is beneficial in females (<a id="xref-ref-87-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Parisi <em>et al.</em> 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Connallon and Clark 2010</a>), the observed enrichment of female-biased genes on the X would be consistent with sexually antagonistic selection because X-linked alleles occur twice as often in females than in males. Similarly, the magnitude of sex-biased gene expression (absolute log<sub>2</sub>FC) was higher in the sex-linked region, as might be expected if levels of gene expression were the result of an escalation of antagonistic gene expression between the sexes. The possibility that sexually antagonistic selection played a role in the evolution of suppressed recombination might be investigated further by seeking sex-linked QTL for sexually antagonistic traits (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Delph <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>), or functional analysis of candidate genes. It would also be valuable to compare expression levels of sex-linked genes with those in a related species with ancestral levels of gene expression, such as orthologs in <em>M. huetii</em> that still lie outside the nonrecombining region of the Y.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2 class="">Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-73">We thank John Russell for setting up the crosses, Jos Käfer for help with the Galaxy workflow for the SEX-DETector analysis, Jerome Goudet for statistical advice, Kamil S.   <div class="region-inner region-postscript-first-inner">
    <div class="block block-panels-mini block-snippet-about-gsa block-panels-mini-snippet-about-gsa odd block-without-title" id="block-panels-mini-snippet-about-gsa">
  <div class="block-inner clearfix">
                
    <div class="content clearfix">
      <div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" id="mini-panel-snippet_about_gsa">
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-snippet pane-snippet-about-gsa" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="snippet snippet-about-gsa" id="snippet-about-gsa">
  
      
  <div class="snippet-content">
    <p><a href="http://www.genetics-gsa.org/" target="_blank"><img alt="GSA" src="/sites/default/files/additional-assets/GSALogoWhite_lg.png" style="width: 225px; height: 95px;" /></a></p>

<p>The Genetics Society of America (GSA), founded in 1931, is the professional membership organization for scientific researchers and educators in the field of genetics. Our members work to advance knowledge in the basic mechanisms of inheritance, from the molecular to the population level.</p>

<p>Online ISSN: 1943-2631</p>
  </div>
